        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.3c07118"><meta property="og:title" content="Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/acsami.3c07118.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="A sodium-ion battery, with a wide operating range, is much cheaper and safer than a lithium battery. Graphene is regarded as a promising carbon material in the preparation of anode materials. However, the large two-dimensional (2D) graphene sheets restrain the cross-plane diffusion of electrolyte ions, limiting the further improvement of rate performance. Herein, a nanohybrid of FeCo2Se4 and holey graphene (FeCo2Se4/HG) has been successfully prepared by the synchronism of pore creation and active material growth. Specifically, FeCo-oxide nanoparticles serve as the etching agents, generating in-plane nanoholes and subsequently converted into FeCo2Se4. The nanoholes provide a high density of cross-plane diffusion channels for sodium ions, serving as ionic diffusion shortcuts between different graphene layers to accelerate ion transport across the entire electrode. The unique architecture endows FeCo2Se4/HG with superior rate capability (411.2 mA h g–1 at 20 A g–1) and a specific capacity of 432.4 mA h g–1 at 2.0 A g–1 after 2000 cycles with a capacity retention rate of 92.4%. Therefore, pore engineering makes it possible for holey graphene-based electrodes to achieve outstanding rate performance and superb cycling durability."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/acsami.3c07118.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931883114e6354b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931883114e6354b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'd0aefe39-3fea-4ade-9668-254605d950d6'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/lazy-imports-6922fe6a4ca6b2e0cd9a.js"></script><script type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931883114e6354b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931883114e6354b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bimetal Oxide Reduction-Induced Perforation Strategy for Preparing a Multi-Microchannel Graphene-Based Anode Material with Rapid Sodium-Ion Diffusion", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.3c07118", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

R. China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Xuejie++Wang" data-id="article_author_search">Xuejie Wang</a></span></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Wenxue Yan</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Wenxue Yan</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">Laboratory of Solar Fuel, Faculty of Materials Science and Chemistry, China University of Geosciences, 68 Jincheng Street, Wuhan 430078, P. data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/medium/am3c07118_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07118&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">A sodium-ion battery, with a wide operating range, is much cheaper and safer than a lithium battery. Graphene is regarded as a promising carbon material in the preparation of anode materials. However, the large two-dimensional (2D) graphene sheets restrain the cross-plane diffusion of electrolyte ions, limiting the further improvement of rate performance. Herein, a nanohybrid of FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> and holey graphene (FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>/HG) has been successfully prepared by the synchronism of pore creation and active material growth. Specifically, FeCo-oxide nanoparticles serve as the etching agents, generating in-plane nanoholes and subsequently converted into FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>. The nanoholes provide a high density of cross-plane diffusion channels for sodium ions, serving as ionic diffusion shortcuts between different graphene layers to accelerate ion transport across the entire electrode. The unique architecture endows FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>/HG with superior rate capability (411.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and a specific capacity of 432.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles with a capacity retention rate of 92.4%. Therefore, pore engineering makes it possible for holey graphene-based electrodes to achieve outstanding rate performance and superb cycling durability.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D43691%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTao%2BLiu%252C%2BYu%2BYang%252C%2BXuejie%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D37%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c07118%26title%3DBimetal%2BOxide%2BReduction-Induced%2BPerforation%2BStrategy%2Bfor%2BPreparing%2Ba%2BMulti-Microchannel%2BGraphene-Based%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bwith%2BRapid%2BSodium-Ion%2BDiffusion%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D43701%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion"> Diffusion </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells"> Electrochemical cells </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles"> Nanoparticles </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Holey+Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_holey%20graphene">holey graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Feco2se4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_FeCo2Se4">FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanohole&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanohole">nanohole</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical energy storage technologies have attracted wide attention to utilizing sustainable energy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Especially, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have become the dominating energy storage system with widespread applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 5 to 8">(5−8)</a> However, the unevenly distributed and limited lithium resources in the earth’s crust inevitably lead to high costs. In this regard, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), with the cost-effectiveness of abundant sodium resources, as well as the electrochemical similarity with LIBs, have shown their great potential in terms of large-scale energy storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 9 to 14">(9−14)</a> However, SIBs display low specific capacity and sluggish reaction dynamics arising from the higher redox potentials of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (−2.7 V) compared with Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (−3.0 V) and larger atomic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) to Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> Unlike LIBs that can directly use graphite as the anode, suitable anodes for SIBs are still under exploration. Therefore, research into anode materials with outstanding capacity and prolonged lifespan is crucial.</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism, anode materials are composed of three types: intercalation (carbon- <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> and titanium-based composite <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a>), conversion (transitional metal oxides <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> and chalcogenides <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a>), and alloying (Si, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Ge, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Sn, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> P <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a>). Transition-metal chalcogenides have become prospective anode materials owing to supreme capacity and excellent structural stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a> Among them, transitional metal selenides (TMSs) possess increased electrochemical reaction activity owing to the weaker metal–Se bonds compared with metal–S or metal–O bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> However, their inherent shortcomings, namely, poor electrical conductivity together with significant volume variation and polyselenide dissolution during conversion reactions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> induce abominable rate capability and serious capacity decay. To resolve these, the incorporation of carbonaceous materials and the design of bimetal selenides have been identified as two effective protocols to achieve excellent electrochemical properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> As for the first protocol, excellent stability of cycling more than 1300 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> has been realized by the embedding of MoSe<sub>2</sub> nanosheets into the bilayer hollow carbon shells. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> As for the second one, the three-dimensional (3D) pomegranate-like bimetallic NiCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> modified by N-doped carbon delivered a stable capacity of 433.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> These successful examples inspire the design of carbon-tuned binary TMS.</div><div class="NLM_p">Owing to the extraordinary electric conductivity and mechanical robustness, graphene is deemed a suitable candidate to hybrid with TMS, which can accelerate electron transfer and provide electrochemical active sites, thus improving sodium-ion storage performances. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a> For example, a “sandwich” structure, with N-doped carbon and graphene as the “bread” and MoSe<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as the “stuffing,” was obtained by hydrothermal and polymerization methods, which demonstrated a high capacity of 540 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and long-term cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> However, morphological control of vulnerable nanostructures is not applicable for commercialization. By contrast, the original morphology of two-dimensional graphene-based composites can be well maintained after electrode preparation. Nevertheless, sodium-ion diffusion across the graphene plane is difficult. The introduction of nanoholes on graphene sheets to open longitudinal ion diffusion channels is an efficient strategy to solve this problem, shortening the ion diffusion distance and offering plentiful active sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, holey graphene (HG) decorated by binary-metal selenides (FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>) is designed through a facile method. Moreover, FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were adjacent to the holes of HG nanosheets. The distinctive architecture endows electrode materials with excellent conductivity with boosted electron transfer. Besides, HG, as a carbonaceous matrix, can accommodate the volumetric expansion and enhance the structural robustness. In particular, the abundant in-plane nanopores created on graphene sheets provide longitudinal channels for ion transport. Benefiting from the above merits, the FCS/HG electrode exhibited extraordinary cycling stability of 432.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles (92.4% capacity retention) and superior rate performance of 411.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at an extremely high current density of 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>. When the FCS/HG anode was further combined with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a cathode into sodium-ion full cells, the assembled FCS/HG||NVP full cell also displayed captivating rate capacity and cycling. The impressive electrochemical properties of the FCS/HG anode indicate its potential in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of FCS/HG</h3><div class="NLM_p last">FCS/HG was prepared by chemical bath deposition and two-step calcination. First, 116.4 mg of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O and 88.6 mg of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 20 mL of deionized water, and then the solution was added dropwise into the 50 mL of a GO suspension (3 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) during stirring at room temperature. After ultrasound for 0.5 h, the mixture was stirred continuously for 12 h. Subsequently, 0.3 mL of ammonium hydroxide was added to the mixture. After stirring for another 4 h at 80 °C, the precipitate (FeCo<sub>2</sub>-precursor/GO) was obtained after freeze-drying for 24 h. Second, the FeCo<sub>2</sub>-precursor/GO was calcinated at 800 °C under N<sub>2</sub> for 2 h to obtain a bimetal nanoparticle/HG composite (FeCo<sub>2</sub>/HG). Finally, FeCo<sub>2</sub>/HG was selenized with Se powder at 650 °C for 2 h to obtain FCS/HG. For comparison, FCS/rGO was fabricated by annealing the above-mentioned CoFe-precursor/GO with Se powder under N<sub>2</sub> flow at 650 °C for 2 h.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis procedure of the FCS/HG composite is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. First of all, owing to abundant functional groups on graphene oxide (GO), cobalt and iron ions were homogeneously anchored on the GO nanosheet surface. After subsequent chemical bath deposition with the addition of NH<sub>4</sub>OH, FeCo-precursor nanoparticles as double hydroxides were grown on GO (FeCo<sub>2</sub>-precursors/GO). Afterward, FeCo<sub>2</sub>-precursors/GO was converted into FeCo<sub>2</sub>/HG by calcination in the N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. During the heating process of calcination, the FeCo<sub>2</sub>-precursor was decomposed and assembled into binary oxide nanocrystals with critical nuclei size by ion rearrangement. Then, these nanocrystals grew into nanoparticles with increased size. When the temperature reached 800 °C, the oxide nanoparticles were further reduced to FeCo<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles by C at high temperature, and correspondingly, nanoholes were generated in the graphene sheets. Finally, FeCo<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were converted into FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on holey graphene sheets after selenylation.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/medium/am3c07118_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Illustration sketch for the synthesis procedure of FCS/HG.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07118&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology evolution of FCS/HG was assessed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM). FE-SEM images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) displayed two-dimensional nanosheets of GO with smooth and plicate surfaces. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b, there is no obvious morphology change from GO to FeCo-precursor/GO. After redox etching at high temperatures, ample nanoholes were generated close to the FeCo<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d). As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e,f, FCS/HG shared similar micromorphology to FeCo<sub>2</sub>/HG. For comparison, the composite without nanoholes (FCS/rGO) was also generated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Furthermore, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were further recorded. TEM images of FCS/HG (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,b) displayed that FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were evenly distributed on HG with nanoholes adjacent to them. The pore size of nanoholes was about 50–100 nm. The formation of in-plane holes is based on the thermal etching method, so the pore size of in-plane holes is closely related to the reduction temperature from FeCo-precursors to FeCo/HG. When the temperature was below 800 °C, the in-plane holes were hard to generate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>). By contrast, when the temperature was above 800 °C, the FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were severely aggregated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>). High-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d) disclosed the lattice spacing of 2.67 Å, in line with (111) crystal facets of monoclinic FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>. The edge of the FCS nanoparticle displayed a clear lattice fringe that belonged to FCS, confirming that there was no carbon layer on FCS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings of FCS/HG (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e–g) further demonstrated the homogeneous dispersion of Fe, Se, C, and Co elements in the FCS/HG hybrid.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/medium/am3c07118_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a, b) FE-SEM images of FeCo-precursor/rGO. (c, d) FE-SEM images of FeCo<sub>2</sub>/HG. (e, f) FE-SEM images of FCS/HG.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07118&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/medium/am3c07118_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a, b) TEM images, (c, d) HR-TEM images, and (e-g) EDX elemental mapping of FCS/HG.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07118&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The crystalline structure transformation was examined through X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). The weak peak of FeCo<sub>2</sub>-precursor/GO, located at 23.5°, was attributed to the characteristic peak of GO. While no apparent characteristic signal of the FeCo-precursor was witnessed, resulting from the ultrafine nanocrystal of the FeCo-precursor. After calcination at 800 °C under N<sub>2</sub> protection, the emerging peaks at 44.7 and 65.2° were assigned to FeCo<sub>2</sub> metal particles, resulting from the thermal reduction by carbon. All of the peaks of FCS/HG corresponded to the diffraction peaks of FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>, suggesting the successful selenylation from FeCo<sub>2</sub> to FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles. The XRD pattern (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) of the control sample, FCS/rGO, also indicated the successful transformation from the CoFe-precursor to FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>. The Raman spectra of two hybrids are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. And two peaks situated at 1346 and 1586 cm<sup>–1</sup> signify D and G bands of carbon, respectively. <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> represents the intensity ratio of the D band to the G band, suggesting the amount of defective carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> After etching nanoholes on graphene planes, <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> rose from 1.0 to 1.3, suggesting that more defective carbon originated from in-plane holes of FCS/HG. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed to measure the carbon ratio and thermostability of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The mass of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO slightly increased with increasing temperature to 400 °C, attributing to the oxidation of FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> into FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>4–<i>x</i></sub> and SeO<sub>2</sub>. Subsequently, obvious weight loss occurred between 400 and 600 °C arising from the decomposition of FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>4–<i>x</i></sub> into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and the evaporation of SeO<sub>2</sub>, together with the consumption of graphene. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 26, 43 and 44">(26,43,44)</a> As a result, the carbon contents of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO were calculated to be about 13.04 and 25.05%, respectively. FCS/HG has a lower carbon content than FCS/GO, resulting from graphene consumption during the formation of in-plane holes. Moreover, N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption was conducted to investigate the specific surface area and pore distribution of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c, the typical type-IV isotherms with type-H3 hysteresis loops demonstrate the presence of mesopores. Based on that, the specific surface area of FCS/HG (54.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) is inferior to that of FCS/rGO (66.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) because of the lower graphene content. However, the pore volume of FCS/HG (0.158 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) was higher than that of FCS/rGO (0.146 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), arising from the existence of nanoholes on graphene sheets. The ample nanoholes foster active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and boost the diffusion of electrolytes.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/medium/am3c07118_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XRD patterns of FeCo-precursor/rGO, FeCo<sub>2</sub>/HG, and FCS/HG. (b) Raman spectra and (c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption profiles and pore distribution of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO. High-resolution XPS spectra of (d) Fe 2p, (e) Co 2p, and (f) Se 3d for FCS/HG and FCS/rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07118&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) were also collected to investigate the chemical shift and elemental constituents of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO. The survey spectra of both samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>) showed the existence of C, Fe, O, Co, and Se elements, while the existence of O is due to partial oxidation of the samples by air. FCS/HG and FCS/rGO had similar chemical element states. The high-resolution spectrum of Fe 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d) presented four peaks at 711.2/714.1 eV for Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> of Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 724/727.9 eV for Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> of Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. Another two peaks at 718.5 and 732.3 eV were, respectively, assigned to the satellite peaks of Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub>. Co 2p showed four peaks situated at 778.5/793.3 and 781.4/797.2 eV that were, respectively, assigned to Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>2+</sup> of Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, while two peaks located at 786.4 and 802.7 eV were, respectively, indexed to the satellite peaks of Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e). The Se 3d spectrum presents two peaks at 54.3 and 55 eV that were ascribed to Se 3d<sub>2/5</sub> and Se 3d<sub>2/3</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 45 to 47">(45−47)</a> Additionally, the peak of 59.4 eV was indexed to SeO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, arising from the surface oxidation of the composite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The C 1s spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>) showed three peaks at 284.8, 285.6 eV, and 288.3 eV, which were assigned to sp<sup>2</sup> C, C―O, and C═O, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">FCS/HG, together with FCS/rGO, as an SIB anode, were evaluated by half cells at the voltage ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves for the first four cycles of FCS/HG are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. During the first cathodic scanning process, the sharp peak that emerged at 1.0 V was attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles with the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film induced by irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> And the broad peak at 0.45 V was assigned to the subsequent conversion reaction that FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> converted into Na<sub>2</sub>Se and metal iron and cobalt. Two overlapped peaks at 1.7 and 1.8 V were attributed to the desodiation reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>Se to FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> during the subsequent anodic scan. In the subsequent cycles, three cathodic peaks observed at 0.72, 1.2, and 1.46 eV can be attributed to the multistep transformation of metal selenide to Na<sub>2</sub>Se with sodiation. In addition, three oxidic peaks that appeared at 1.52, 1.78, and 1.92 eV were ascribed to metal iron and cobalt reacted with Na<sub>2</sub>Se into FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">These transformation steps can be described as follows:</div><div class="NLM_p">In the discharging process<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-32" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-33">−</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-34"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-35" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">→</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-36"><span class="MJXp-mrow" class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-392">2</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-393">x</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-394" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: id="MJXp-Span-400">e</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-401" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-402">−</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-6"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>Fe</mi><mo>+</mo><mn>2</mn><mi>Co</mi><mo>+</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>4</mn><mo>−</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>−</mo><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mi>Se</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>FeC</mi><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>8</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>2</mn><mi>x</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>2</mn><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>8</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>2</mn><mi>x</mi><mo>−</mo><mn>2</mn><mi>y</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the peaks near 0 V were indicated to be Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation into graphene. The first four galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles of FCS/HG at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> were depicted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). In the first cycle, FCS/HG demonstrated an unprecedented discharge/charge capacity of 741.8/565.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 76.2%. The faded capacity was mainly caused by the formation of the SEI film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Galvanostatic discharge–charge (GCD) curves in the subsequent cycles almost coincided with the second one, indicating the good stability of the FCS/HG hybrid during cycles. Furthermore, the charge/discharge voltage plateaus in GCD profiles agree with the oxidation/reduction peaks in CV curves in all cycles. The GCD profiles after hundreds of cycles (100th, 200th, 500th) almost overlapped, demonstrating the excellent stability of the FCS/HG electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/medium/am3c07118_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) GCD curves of FCS/HG. (b) Cycling performance and (c) rate performance of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO. (d) Rate performance comparison of FCS/HG with the literature. (e) Long-cycling performance of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/asset/images/large/am3c07118_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c07118&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The stability of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO was evaluated and is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b. After 200 repetitive charging and discharging, the FCS/HG composite possessed better cycle performance, which can deliver a good reversible capacity of 584 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with no obvious capacity deterioration. In contrast, the capacity of FCS/rGO underwent obvious decay to 533.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Besides benefiting from the designed nanostructure, the FCS/HG composite also demonstrates superior rate capability than FCS/rGO at increasing current densities. The differences in capacity gradually expanded with increasing current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). The FCS/HG exhibited a capacity of 544.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, it still possessed a high capacity of 425.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10,000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> along with 411.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 20,000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, outperforming FCS/rGO (451.3, 311.9, and 266.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively). Moreover, a high capacity of 509.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was attained by FCS/HG when the current density went back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Its superior rate performance reflected that the expedited diffusion is due to the existence of in-plane nanoholes in FCS/HG. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d, the rate performance of FCS/HG surpassed most reported TMS-based anode materials (e.g., NiCoSe@NC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> NCS-6@NPG, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> Zn-Co-Se@NDC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> NFC@NC, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> (NiFe)Sex-GC-CNT, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> ZnSe–FeSe<sub>2</sub>/RGO, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> ZnSe⊂N-C@MoSe<sub>2</sub>/rGO, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> Cu<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Se nanocrystals, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> and FeS<sub>1.6</sub>Se<sub>0.4</sub> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a>), indicating the structure advantages of in-plane nanoholes on graphene sheets with binary-metal selenides in the FCS/HG hybrid.</div><div class="NLM_p">The long-cycle stabilities of FCS/HG and FCS/rGO are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e. FCS/HG delivered a charge/discharge capacity of 468.2/611.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 76.5%. And it achieved a stable charge capacity of 432.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (92.4% capacity was reserved from the initial cycle), together with a Coulombic efficiency of over 99.4%. This rapid capacity decay that occurred at the initial cycles was attributed to electrolyte decomposition and the generation of the SEI film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> For comparison, FCS/rGO only achieved 294.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (capacity retention rate of 71.6%). The unusual increase in capacity for FCS/HG in the middle and later stages can be attributed to the temperature variation of the testing environment. The capacity of FCS/HG is sensitive to environmental changes. Moreover, FCS/HG could also attain 306.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 21.9% capacity decay (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c07118/suppl_file/am3c07118_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The eminent cycle durability of FCS/HG indicated that the nanoholes could strengthen the synergistic effect between FeCo<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> and graphene.</div><div class="NLM_p">For FCS/HG to exhibit outstanding capacity as well as rate/cycle performance, it is essential to study the capacitive effect together with the kinetic process of FCS/HG by CV tests. From 0.2 to 1.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a), CV profiles had similar shapes and the cathode and anode peaks shifted toward opposite directions at increased scan rates. Especially the peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) followed the formula<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq7"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-403"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-404"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-405"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-406"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-407">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-408"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-409" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-410"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-411">a</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-412"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-413" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-414">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-415" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-416">b</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-7"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>a</mi><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>b</mi></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(7)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq8"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-417"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-418"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-419"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-420"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-421">log</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-422" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-423">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-424"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-425" style="margin-left: 0.333em; height: 0em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-429">log</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-430" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-431">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-432" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-433">log</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-434" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-435">a</span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-8"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace=".25em">⁡</mo><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>b</mi><mspace width="0.25em"></mspace><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace=".25em">⁡</mo><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace=".25em">⁡</mo><mi>a</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(8)</span></div></div>where <i>i</i> and <i>v</i> are variable quantities and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants. When the <i>b</i> value approaches 0.5, it indicates a dominating diffusion-controlled process. If <i>b</i> approaches 1.0, the capacitive process dominates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 59 and 60">(59,60)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b displayed the linear relationship curves about <i>i</i> and <i>v</i> according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="formula 2">formula 2</a>, and the <i>b</i> values for peak 1, peak 2, and peak 3 were calculated to be 0.63, 0.59, and 0.72, respectively. The <i>b</i> value ranged from 0.5 to 1.0, disclosing the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of capacitive and diffusion processes. Furthermore, the ratio that the capacitive process accounts for at different scan rates can be calculated through <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq9" aria-label="eq 9">eq 9</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq9"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-436"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-437"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-438"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-439"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-440">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-441"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-442" style="margin-left: 0.333em;
